PRIDE & PREJUDICE.	 34.24156922046106
CHAPTER I.	 254.0372352719546
It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single man in possession	 39.41435990153084
of a good fortune, must be in want of a wife.	 25.656511443153782
However little known the feelings or views of such a man may be on his	 49.5369630378193
first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so well fixed in the minds	 57.48788141824522
of the surrounding families, that he is considered as the rightful	 19.911652443951205
property of some one or other of their daughters.	 84.19325410368509
"My dear Mr. Bennet," said his lady to him one day, "have you heard that	 41.267973756804885
Netherfield Park is let at last?"	 76.91443445241165
Mr. Bennet replied that he had not.	 21.07908518621704
"But it is," returned she; "for Mrs. Long has just been here, and she	 59.688269988582356
told me all about it."	 69.58477688442264
Mr. Bennet made no answer.	 15.009735574554794
"Do not you want to know who has taken it?" cried his wife impatiently.	 31.29262405739983
"_You_ want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing it."	 12.982509623548282
This was invitation enough.	 29.66737739162406
"Why, my dear, you must know, Mrs. Long says that Netherfield is taken	 30.067799389392547
by a young man of large fortune from the north of England; that he came	 13.636131596012758
down on Monday in a chaise and four to see the place, and was so much	 67.89990184631064
delighted with it that he agreed with Mr. Morris immediately; that he is	 49.66377095264621
to take possession before Michaelmas, and some of his servants are to be	 47.17211173199242
in the house by the end of next week."	 28.240253056277446
"What is his name?"	 61.078828980855015
"Bingley."	 454.778358823175
"Is he married or single?"	 71.44877454881407
"Oh! single, my dear, to be sure! A single man of large fortune; four	 24.539554677074314
or five thousand a year. What a fine thing for our girls!"	 64.38649893541037
"How so? how can it affect them?"	 105.84444367941983
"My dear Mr. Bennet," replied his wife, "how can you be so tiresome! You	 14.058590070699848
must know that I am thinking of his marrying one of them."	 22.862879049868354
"Is that his design in settling here?"	 120.45524564947411
"Design! nonsense, how can you talk so! But it is very likely that he	 23.38880237368118
_may_ fall in love with one of them, and therefore you must visit him as	 38.88431763681387
soon as he comes."	 43.65180429823733
"I see no occasion for that. You and the girls may go, or you may send	 36.30179052819944
them by themselves, which perhaps will be still better, for as you are	 45.46175760137096
as handsome as any of them, Mr. Bingley might like you the best of the	 44.93894226185378
party."	 1293.9488738680216
"My dear, you flatter me. I certainly _have_ had my share of beauty, but	 23.475290826860746
I do not pretend to be any thing extraordinary now. When a woman has	 17.809999447145824
five grown up daughters, she ought to give over thinking of her own	 67.92342459325772
beauty."	 2212.078411437485
"In such cases, a woman has not often much beauty to think of."	 21.44163844936354
"But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Mr. Bingley when he comes into	 21.08023423452625
the neighbourhood."	 69.61857966815941
"It is more than I engage for, I assure you."	 16.920527060691878
"But consider your daughters. Only think what an establishment it would	 56.957968746207555
be for one of them. Sir William and Lady Lucas are determined to go,	 26.934182078107288
merely on that account, for in general you know they visit no new	 157.4402327240746
comers. Indeed you must go, for it will be impossible for _us_ to visit	 24.430385384063005
him, if you do not."	 28.411559813716426
"You are over scrupulous surely. I dare say Mr. Bingley will be very	 16.057641603958665
glad to see you; and I will send a few lines by you to assure him of my	 21.230427252801043
hearty consent to his marrying which ever he chuses of the girls; though	 38.69321140045496
I must throw in a good word for my little Lizzy."	 36.45862719184189
"I desire you will do no such thing. Lizzy is not a bit better than the	 14.868307207937667
others; and I am sure she is not half so handsome as Jane, nor half so	 23.58928536514397
good humoured as Lydia. But you are always giving _her_ the	 45.49181894829794
preference."	 2651.652844139274
"They have none of them much to recommend them," replied he; "they are	 46.166541946911906
all silly and ignorant like other girls; but Lizzy has something more of	 55.27858045770332
quickness than her sisters."	 32.9001992087311
"Mr. Bennet, how can you abuse your own children in such a way? You take	 20.159124598229283
delight in vexing me. You have no compassion on my poor nerves."	 32.332824031972486
"You mistake me, my dear. I have a high respect for your nerves. They	 25.056886003237796
are my old friends. I have heard you mention them with consideration	 41.249790073989445
these twenty years at least."	 31.3938433226576
"Ah! you do not know what I suffer."	 12.330704107545943
"But I hope you will get over it, and live to see many young men of four	 25.278216315965757
thousand a year come into the neighbourhood."	 41.33599248237336
"It will be no use to us, if twenty such should come since you will not	 36.666226388136344
visit them."	 134.35418455248612
"Depend upon it, my dear, that when there are twenty, I will visit them	 23.515581247473513
all."	 281.1320469278742
Mr. Bennet was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour,	 22.028684693399505
reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three and twenty years had	 17.829747022575404
been insufficient to make his wife understand his character. _Her_ mind	 47.51475170186869
was less difficult to develope. She was a woman of mean understanding,	 33.57152668100196
little information, and uncertain temper. When she was discontented she	 38.113620085365
fancied herself nervous. The business of her life was to get her	 33.32778519431317
daughters married; its solace was visiting and news.	 53.07844855872076
CHAPTER II.	 12.911953956184709
Mr. Bennet was among the earliest of those who waited on Mr. Bingley. He	 15.96405506564304
had always intended to visit him, though to the last always assuring his	 33.77751075357959
wife that he should not go; and till the evening after the visit was	 27.17981274653174
paid, she had no knowledge of it. It was then disclosed in the following	 34.27806993640263
manner. Observing his second daughter employed in trimming a hat, he	 27.642761590165986
suddenly addressed her with,	 39.00280111078705
"I hope Mr. Bingley will like it Lizzy."	 50.924588900583515
"We are not in a way to know _what_ Mr. Bingley likes," said her mother	 18.02809835802892
resentfully, "since we are not to visit."	 27.080206802078944
"But you forget, mama," said Elizabeth, "that we shall meet him at the	 28.403503896643286
assemblies, and that Mrs. Long has promised to introduce him."	 21.534553737013837
"I do not believe Mrs. Long will do any such thing. She has two nieces	 20.707738208385233
of her own. She is a selfish, hypocritical woman, and I have no opinion	 22.50687656394876
of her."	 24.152927322591697
"No more have I," said Mr. Bennet; "and I am glad to find that you do	 23.7387341024638
not depend on her serving you."	 58.525075058537475
Mrs. Bennet deigned not to make any reply; but unable to contain	 33.943049381194506
herself, began scolding one of her daughters.	 71.49390860803422
"Don't keep coughing so, Kitty, for heaven's sake! Have a little	 75.87111844347021
compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces."	 30.679661463947074
"Kitty has no discretion in her coughs," said her father; "she times	 49.85214125842148
them ill."	 412.72513702868105
"I do not cough for my own amusement," replied Kitty fretfully.	 25.064976424470238
"When is your next ball to be, Lizzy?"	 72.24981994127887
"To-morrow fortnight."	 130.47857536901483
"Aye, so it is," cried her mother, "and Mrs. Long does not come back	 16.70452245173428
till the day before; so, it will be impossible for her to introduce him,	 26.234594082335466
for she will not know him herself."	 73.40325897752246
"Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your friend, and introduce	 26.489705426927156
Mr. Bingley to _her_."	 44.65202675071859
"Impossible, Mr. Bennet, impossible, when I am not acquainted with him	 48.89668118763648
myself; how can you be so teazing?"	 19.268862254039238
"I honour your circumspection. A fortnight's acquaintance is certainly	 35.816075871766394
very little. One cannot know what a man really is by the end of a	 37.48466141338808
fortnight. But if _we_ do not venture, somebody else will; and after	 25.43786241080946
all, Mrs. Long and her nieces must stand their chance; and therefore, as	 55.30903995850956
she will think it an act of kindness, if you decline the office, I will	 41.00476956198487
take it on myself."	 247.16049754733828
The girls stared at their father. Mrs. Bennet said only, "Nonsense,	 79.18109315994461
nonsense!"	 8789.441735227052
"What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?" cried he. "Do	 39.0874004618612
you consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that is laid on	 24.779528583289668
them, as nonsense? I cannot quite agree with you _there_. What say you,	 63.14521088765278
Mary? for you are a young lady of deep reflection I know, and read great	 55.782109327770215
books, and make extracts."	 127.26558336294548
Mary wished to say something very sensible, but knew not how.	 27.68485453803499
"While Mary is adjusting her ideas," he continued, "let us return to Mr.	 61.34786128139574
Bingley."	 454.778358823175
"I am sick of Mr. Bingley," cried his wife.	 16.863874367712558
"I am sorry to hear _that_; but why did not you tell me so before? If I	 17.08168802358164
had known as much this morning, I certainly would not have called on	 36.65903723242531
him. It is very unlucky; but as I have actually paid the visit, we	 25.505543769263085
cannot escape the acquaintance now."	 165.2363581669323
The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Mrs.	 47.630829334468956
Bennet perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy	 40.76830615453486
was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the	 22.29191286176887
while.	 485.1868114488692
"How good it was in you, my dear Mr. Bennet! But I knew I should	 34.915794563406784
persuade you at last. I was sure you loved your girls too well to	 43.363886446934465
neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased I am! and it is such a	 47.249914195344004
good joke, too, that you should have gone this morning, and never said	 55.28175998986903
a word about it till now."	 92.3458979248966
"Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you chuse," said Mr. Bennet; and,	 31.229517995285295
as he spoke, he left the room, fatigued with the raptures of his wife.	 32.702083970871605
"What an excellent father you have, girls," said she, when the door was	 27.985947960645127
shut. "I do not know how you will ever make him amends for his kindness;	 22.008594118530844
or me either, for that matter. At our time of life, it is not so	 79.95296489106504
pleasant I can tell you, to be making new acquaintance every day; but	 51.296408249742505
for your sakes, we would do any thing. Lydia, my love, though you _are_	 54.932798560171896
the youngest, I dare say Mr. Bingley will dance with you at the next	 31.0154358620278
ball."	 2287.2042375107944
"Oh!" said Lydia stoutly, "I am not afraid; for though I _am_ the	 24.09820750656037
youngest, I'm the tallest."	 50.50868250449054
The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon he would	 19.52134705594832
return Mr. Bennet's visit, and determining when they should ask him to	 37.12999418770201
dinner.	 2091.015003694601
CHAPTER III.	 12.911953956184709
Not all that Mrs. Bennet, however, with the assistance of her five	 59.165620212143246
daughters, could ask on the subject was sufficient to draw from her	 35.18220968356949
husband any satisfactory description of Mr. Bingley. They attacked him	 53.746964303968845
in various ways; with barefaced questions, ingenious suppositions, and	 42.75031265053046
distant surmises; but he eluded the skill of them all; and they were at	 18.71661021169711
last obliged to accept the second-hand intelligence of their neighbour	 30.445980571964157
Lady Lucas. Her report was highly favourable. Sir William had been	 55.476977852829854
delighted with him. He was quite young, wonderfully handsome, extremely	 52.13870338631428
agreeable, and to crown the whole, he meant to be at the next assembly	 25.65791598988965
with a large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To be fond of	 21.100335370952028
dancing was a certain step towards falling in love; and very lively	 47.982207474193686
hopes of Mr. Bingley's heart were entertained.	 42.64030303240259
"If I can but see one of my daughters happily settled at Netherfield,"	 37.42828967093633
said Mrs. Bennet to her husband, "and all the others equally well	 18.262296398958238
married, I shall have nothing to wish for."	 34.48965000755792
In a few days Mr. Bingley returned Mr. Bennet's visit, and sat about ten	 43.07101320781279
minutes with him in his library. He had entertained hopes of being	 24.612773782340923
admitted to a sight of the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard	 36.60389940097333
much; but he saw only the father. The ladies were somewhat more	 87.66293768333394
fortunate, for they had the advantage of ascertaining from an upper	 47.39578245533543
window, that he wore a blue coat and rode a black horse.	 19.238458038728425
An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; and already had	 44.60098566203735
Mrs. Bennet planned the courses that were to do credit to her	 29.683818403620588
housekeeping, when an answer arrived which deferred it all. Mr. Bingley	 31.369485462947893
was obliged to be in town the following day, and consequently unable to	 16.50038602738376
accept the honour of their invitation, &c. Mrs. Bennet was quite	 31.51761435420375
disconcerted. She could not imagine what business he could have in town	 37.965557344857295
so soon after his arrival in Hertfordshire; and she began to fear that	 26.524353492994656
he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never	 110.90534172216312
settled at Netherfield as he ought to be. Lady Lucas quieted her fears a	 36.549485105196084
little by starting the idea of his being gone to London only to get a	 26.676064051992213
large party for the ball; and a report soon followed that Mr. Bingley	 45.66162211276318
was to bring twelve ladies and seven gentlemen with him to the assembly.	 75.13376694178802
The girls grieved over such a number of ladies; but were comforted the	 44.07042199707454
day before the ball by hearing, that instead of twelve, he had brought	 40.08420326311507
only six with him from London, his five sisters and a cousin. And when	 49.64282144781751
the party entered the assembly room, it consisted of only five	 66.80217719589464
altogether; Mr. Bingley, his two sisters, the husband of the eldest, and	 77.87146730557707
another young man.	 39.65548930089471
Mr. Bingley was good looking and gentleman-like; he had a pleasant	 68.78361824114903
countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. His sisters were fine women,	 63.24939728323249
with an air of decided fashion. His brother-in-law, Mr. Hurst, merely	 34.25462346015557
looked the gentleman; but his friend Mr. Darcy soon drew the attention	 57.40712589582219
of the room by his fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien; and	 25.772923397836273
the report which was in general circulation within five minutes after	 27.816445188578314
his entrance, of his having ten thousand a year. The gentlemen	 52.88657399921527
pronounced him to be a fine figure of a man, the ladies declared he was	 22.575853340636343
much handsomer than Mr. Bingley, and he was looked at with great	 38.377940738588364
admiration for about half the evening, till his manners gave a disgust	 49.17306670320559
which turned the tide of his popularity; for he was discovered to be	 24.37228510817565
proud, to be above his company, and above being pleased; and not all his	 91.80862424211307
large estate in Derbyshire could then save him from having a most	 66.36491442646935
forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared	 26.880428320622507
with his friend.	 54.9785897173282
Mr. Bingley had soon made himself acquainted with all the principal	 31.036340726056682
people in the room; he was lively and unreserved, danced every dance,	 50.22320728979559
was angry that the ball closed so early, and talked of giving one	 41.48672022668133
himself at Netherfield. Such amiable qualities must speak for	 98.50822707404075
themselves. What a contrast between him and his friend! Mr. Darcy danced	 29.119968929023184
only once with Mrs. Hurst and once with Miss Bingley, declined being	 31.648728429277938
introduced to any other lady, and spent the rest of the evening in	 39.56387562781886
walking about the room, speaking occasionally to one of his own party.	 26.923105045364156
His character was decided. He was the proudest, most disagreeable man in	 20.294088814983773
the world, and every body hoped that he would never come there again.	 28.9558654411204
Amongst the most violent against him was Mrs. Bennet, whose dislike of	 58.060630492978994
his general behaviour, was sharpened into particular resentment, by his	 49.60492428732499
having slighted one of her daughters.	 45.841773469217486
Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged, by the scarcity of gentlemen, to sit	 33.42109751851832
down for two dances; and during part of that time, Mr. Darcy had been	 28.22472545507445
standing near enough for her to overhear a conversation between him and	 24.70143939924412
Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes, to press his	 27.641338423644637
friend to join it.	 94.99075844671707
"Come, Darcy," said he, "I must have you dance. I hate to see you	 55.050522519071876
standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. You had much better	 105.54440689979182
dance."	 1840.0404837693914
"I certainly shall not. You know how I detest it, unless I am	 21.809387503866443
particularly acquainted with my partner. At such an assembly as this, it	 38.287810816983914
would be insupportable. Your sisters are engaged, and there is not	 28.7723171529428
another woman in the room, whom it would not be a punishment to me to	 20.778446697901717
stand up with."	 80.5675444619167
"I would not be so fastidious as you are," cried Bingley, "for a	 20.08309646260528
kingdom! Upon my honour, I never met with so many pleasant girls in my	 12.991909582973795
life, as I have this evening; and there are several of them you see	 49.3991867573222
uncommonly pretty."	 199.24784437604512
"_You_ are dancing with the only handsome girl in the room," said Mr.	 31.362902614900825
Darcy, looking at the eldest Miss Bennet.	 44.430073972041264
"Oh! she is the most beautiful creature I ever beheld! But there is one	 36.30282403242766
of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I	 37.92288706834349
dare say, very agreeable. Do let me ask my partner to introduce you."	 30.761149318757454
"Which do you mean?" and turning round, he looked for a moment at	 40.048583278380974
Elizabeth, till catching her eye, he withdrew his own and coldly said,	 37.760356148600025
"She is tolerable; but not handsome enough to tempt _me_; and I am in no	 20.909492591777955
humour at present to give consequence to young ladies who are slighted	 51.53090496915875
by other men. You had better return to your partner and enjoy her	 33.53833383012813
smiles, for you are wasting your time with me."	 46.930344940524094
Mr. Bingley followed his advice. Mr. Darcy walked off; and Elizabeth	 53.889291823386735
remained with no very cordial feelings towards him. She told the story	 54.95843742053186
however with great spirit among her friends; for she had a lively,	 41.333114933660546
playful disposition, which delighted in any thing ridiculous.	 55.133595941605165
The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family. Mrs.	 14.359128433631849
Bennet had seen her eldest daughter much admired by the Netherfield	 29.17400302811821
party. Mr. Bingley had danced with her twice, and she had been	 25.58054785251728
distinguished by his sisters. Jane was as much gratified by this, as her	 36.74382500858968
mother could be, though in a quieter way. Elizabeth felt Jane's	 53.437695935598775
pleasure. Mary had heard herself mentioned to Miss Bingley as the most	 40.87203325611939
accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Catherine and Lydia had been	 27.243952173206
fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they	 46.59832823574885
had yet learnt to care for at a ball. They returned therefore in good	 45.537254029397175
spirits to Longbourn, the village where they lived, and of which they	 75.82727802497998
were the principal inhabitants. They found Mr. Bennet still up. With a	 43.574359742239444
book he was regardless of time; and on the present occasion he had a	 29.63072429703033
good deal of curiosity as to the event of an evening which had raised	 26.394110740269944
such splendid expectations. He had rather hoped that all his wife's	 19.365595623689202
views on the stranger would be disappointed; but he soon found that he	 39.62245078292434
had a very different story to hear.	 18.04266650811669
"Oh! my dear Mr. Bennet," as she entered the room, "we have had a most	 19.17590011613258
delightful evening, a most excellent ball. I wish you had been there.	 46.26129398822325
Jane was so admired, nothing could be like it. Every body said how well	 83.41213367859179
she looked; and Mr. Bingley thought her quite beautiful, and danced with	 41.69735792948601
her twice. Only think of _that_ my dear; he actually danced with her	 42.42304203916288
twice; and she was the only creature in the room that he asked a second	 26.75867709786746
time. First of all, he asked Miss Lucas. I was so vexed to see him stand	 63.150161796444344
up with her; but, however, he did not admire her at all: indeed, nobody	 42.711364949418915
can, you know; and he seemed quite struck with Jane as she was going	 38.744420613447

CHAPTER V.	 12.911953956184709

CHAPTER VII.	 12.911953956184709
Mr. Bennet's property consisted almost entirely in an estate of two	 33.71690733118498
thousand a year, which, unfortunately for his daughters, was entailed in	 50.148422685508365
default of heirs male, on a distant relation; and their mother's	 22.726574776933777
fortune, though ample for her situation in life, could but ill supply	 35.65973133882655
the deficiency of his. Her father had been an attorney in Meryton, and	 18.85592247576617
had left her four thousand pounds.	 38.181014157259035
She had a sister married to a Mr. Philips, who had been a clerk to their	 30.489347819336103
father, and succeeded him in the business, and a brother settled in	 46.0367596704889
London in a respectable line of trade.	 15.759023009248459
The village of Longbourn was only one mile from Meryton; a most	 20.774176042686623
convenient distance for the young ladies, who were usually tempted	 19.277408750162856
thither three or four times a week, to pay their duty to their aunt and	 18.52614123693122

hours and furnish conversation for the evening; and however bare of news	 75.50301025144977

head quarters.	 251.95579933035307
Their visits to Mrs. Philips were now productive of the most interesting	 29.761330575809446

CHAPTER VIII.	 12.911953956184709

Bingley.	 454.778358823175

CHAPTER IX.	 12.911953956184709
Elizabeth passed the chief of the night in her sister's room, and in the	 25.49511773835407

CHAPTER X.	 12.911953956184709
The day passed much as the day before had done. Mrs. Hurst and Miss	 36.412777437717345
Bingley had spent some hours of the morning with the invalid, who	 27.1271805264227

"How delighted Miss Darcy will be to receive such a letter!"	 39.881839442595044
He made no answer.	 15.701992449615815
"You write uncommonly fast."	 40.218992418631515
"You are mistaken. I write rather slowly."	 16.677889454134196

CHAPTER XI.	 12.911953956184709
When the ladies removed after dinner, Elizabeth ran up to her sister,	 33.21631475248739
and seeing her well guarded from cold, attended her into the	 40.2069592406907

at their acquaintance with spirit.	 53.00418846329815

door. He then sat down by her, and talked scarcely to any one else.	 37.25703721315641
Elizabeth, at work in the opposite corner, saw it all with great	 49.368199695021346
delight.	 2021.951306616539
When tea was over, Mr. Hurst reminded his sister-in-law of the	 18.691468860906134

and Mrs. Hurst, principally occupied in playing with her bracelets and	 28.555264873639402
rings, joined now and then in her brother's conversation with Miss	 60.03573439040089
Bennet.	 400.0597342633047

DEAR SIR,	 117.4245714297951

CHAPTER XV.	 12.911953956184709

CHAPTER XVI.	 12.911953956184709
As no objection was made to the young people's engagement with their	 51.098326810902094

despising his fellow-creatures in general, but did not suspect him of	 24.932940626021136

this!"	 179.3298871670305

his boasting one day, at Netherfield, of the implacability of his	 24.335466639398877

hardly be just to him."	 137.72317718393327
Elizabeth was again deep in thought, and after a time exclaimed, "To	 27.652761544362708

CHAPTER XVII.	 12.911953956184709
Elizabeth related to Jane the next day, what had passed between Mr.	 17.125286262371798
Wickham and herself. Jane listened with astonishment and concern;--she	 39.098802169719406
knew not how to believe that Mr. Darcy could be so unworthy of Mr.	 24.100553028395325
Bingley's regard; and yet, it was not in her nature to question the	 30.33024404148621

otherwise explained.	 90.44400343812096
"They have both," said she, "been deceived, I dare say, in some way or	 34.562048824097

CHAPTER XVIII.	 12.911953956184709
Till Elizabeth entered the drawing-room at Netherfield and looked in	 54.93815430428398

"I dare say you will find him very agreeable."	 15.06747451209475

which had been spoken so loud as to be heard by half the room.--Many	 27.77773634448379
stared.--Many smiled; but no one looked more amused than Mr. Bennet	 44.364183470321265
himself, while his wife seriously commended Mr. Collins for having	 35.598767668408314
spoken so sensibly, and observed in a half-whisper to Lady Lucas, that	 77.22206763294399
he was a remarkably clever, good kind of young man.	 23.680927534014877
To Elizabeth it appeared, that had her family made an agreement to	 47.21524561175667
expose themselves as much as they could during the evening, it would	 39.37427855205492
have been impossible for them to play their parts with more spirit, or	 37.51793798207476
finer success; and happy did she think it for Bingley and her sister	 74.19029428068825
that some of the exhibition had escaped his notice, and that his	 27.503205945541193
feelings were not of a sort to be much distressed by the folly which he	 29.5898194153396
must have witnessed. That his two sisters and Mr. Darcy, however, should	 44.81832574150673
have such an opportunity of ridiculing her relations was bad enough, and	 30.47280115275295
she could not determine whether the silent contempt of the gentleman, or	 27.010995913684024
the insolent smiles of the ladies, were more intolerable.	 24.615269109136452
The rest of the evening brought her little amusement. She was teazed by	 18.625506612759292
Mr. Collins, who continued most perseveringly by her side, and though he	 22.704331427153726
could not prevail with her to dance with him again, put it out of her	 25.734612043254536
power to dance with others. In vain did she entreat him to stand up with	 22.082116580427883
somebody else, and offer to introduce him to any young lady in the room.	 21.370006149523235
He assured her that as to dancing, he was perfectly indifferent to it;	 32.05511437532623
that his chief object was by delicate attentions to recommend himself to	 28.69780287749925
her, and that he should therefore make a point of remaining close to her	 32.80526538847134
the whole evening. There was no arguing upon such a project. She owed	 33.36032933153315
her greatest relief to her friend Miss Lucas, who often joined them, and	 16.232968322276704
good-naturedly engaged Mr. Collins's conversation to herself.	 97.24780734195507
She was at least free from the offence of Mr. Darcy's farther notice;	 10.026209377533657
though often standing within a very short distance of her, quite	 23.558741927171383
disengaged, he never came near enough to speak. She felt it to be the	 20.97692178151971
probable consequence of her allusions to Mr. Wickham, and rejoiced in	 21.7123317271723
it.	 110.84070322522837
The Longbourn party were the last of all the company to depart; and by a	 34.413435970299915
manoeuvre of Mrs. Bennet had to wait for their carriages a quarter of	 20.17995493775693
an hour after every body else was gone, which gave them time to see how	 28.11714579817077
heartily they were wished away by some of the family. Mrs. Hurst and her	 31.92392463487154
sister scarcely opened their mouths except to complain of fatigue, and	 41.79722150772729
were evidently impatient to have the house to themselves. They repulsed	 48.55818937636999
every attempt of Mrs. Bennet at conversation, and by so doing, threw a	 34.37519230807553
languor over the whole party, which was very little relieved by the	 11.923565120625518
long speeches of Mr. Collins, who was complimenting Mr. Bingley and his	 22.686498620561107
sisters on the elegance of their entertainment, and the hospitality and	 22.429285920062334
politeness which had marked their behaviour to their guests. Darcy said	 35.20981704895814
nothing at all. Mr. Bennet, in equal silence, was enjoying the scene.	 60.47832703580827
Mr. Bingley and Jane were standing together, a little detached from the	 21.214655428085134
rest, and talked only to each other. Elizabeth preserved as steady a	 52.31139822813066
silence as either Mrs. Hurst or Miss Bingley; and even Lydia was too	 53.484485131627814
much fatigued to utter more than the occasional exclamation of "Lord,	 37.87312889739712
how tired I am!" accompanied by a violent yawn.	 23.52976498217132
When at length they arose to take leave, Mrs. Bennet was most pressingly	 23.929402788425207
civil in her hope of seeing the whole family soon at Longbourn; and	 39.304675463793
addressed herself particularly to Mr. Bingley, to assure him how happy	 41.388853018613766
he would make them, by eating a family dinner with them at any time,	 34.888176223792996
without the ceremony of a formal invitation. Bingley was all grateful	 30.861323993547465
pleasure, and he readily engaged for taking the earliest opportunity of	 38.4284484276664
waiting on her, after his return from London, whither he was obliged to	 40.546917875699336
go the next day for a short time.	 57.67881438721873
Mrs. Bennet was perfectly satisfied; and quitted the house under the	 21.023742761056738
delightful persuasion that, allowing for the necessary preparations of	 27.34692862761048
settlements, new carriages and wedding clothes, she should undoubtedly	 38.53410229247421
see her daughter settled at Netherfield, in the course of three or four	 23.736709532436187
months. Of having another daughter married to Mr. Collins, she thought	 43.44027936719903
with equal certainty, and with considerable, though not equal, pleasure.	 32.18936339692928
Elizabeth was the least dear to her of all her children; and though the	 33.49119798720965
man and the match were quite good enough for _her_, the worth of each	 66.82124560556491
was eclipsed by Mr. Bingley and Netherfield.	 26.577774629379196
CHAPTER XIX.	 12.911953956184709
The next day opened a new scene at Longbourn. Mr. Collins made his	 30.17732586385741
declaration in form. Having resolved to do it without loss of time, as	 35.12454399226566
his leave of absence extended only to the following Saturday, and having	 61.98334484756241
no feelings of diffidence to make it distressing to himself even at the	 40.03679374783529
moment, he set about it in a very orderly manner, with all the	 36.15370638341425
observances which he supposed a regular part of the business. On finding	 21.963735428016527
Mrs. Bennet, Elizabeth, and one of the younger girls together, soon	 41.727610405866855
after breakfast, he addressed the mother in these words,	 40.95481842866293
"May I hope, Madam, for your interest with your fair daughter Elizabeth,	 57.985058967460546
when I solicit for the honour of a private audience with her in the	 25.94066378519648
course of this morning?"	 74.03555495390094
Before Elizabeth had time for any thing but a blush of surprise, Mrs.	 32.85634693727379
Bennet instantly answered,	 106.89178910716961
"Oh dear!--Yes--certainly.--I am sure Lizzy will be very happy--I am	 21.89219665654654
sure she can have no objection.--Come, Kitty, I want you up stairs." And	 52.5466458745163
gathering her work together, she was hastening away, when Elizabeth	 54.902769660706134
called out,	 82.90232639440518
"Dear Ma'am, do not go.--I beg you will not go.--Mr. Collins must excuse	 30.995979076436353
me.--He can have nothing to say to me that any body need not hear. I am	 21.44795543982817
going away myself."	 107.83532473622483

upon Elizabeth's seeming really, with vexed and embarrassed looks, about	 110.91167171817668
to escape, she added, "Lizzy, I _insist_ upon your staying and hearing	 60.89279722001218
Mr. Collins."	 18.726384204252756
Elizabeth would not oppose such an injunction--and a moment's	 28.414158769293536
consideration making her also sensible that it would be wisest to get it	 37.596090634803105
over as soon and as quietly as possible, she sat down again, and tried	 34.32267325068675
to conceal by incessant employment the feelings which were divided	 44.04560546038809
between distress and diversion. Mrs. Bennet and Kitty walked off, and as	 34.4629319047888

"Believe me, my dear Miss Elizabeth, that your modesty, so far from	 21.5449122314272
doing you any disservice, rather adds to your other perfections. You	 59.747955833899915
would have been less amiable in my eyes had there _not_ been this little	 51.06382647277992
unwillingness; but allow me to assure you that I have your respected	 13.589479530217146
mother's permission for this address. You can hardly doubt the purport	 31.7602875418799
of my discourse, however your natural delicacy may lead you to	 27.027694113663678
dissemble; my attentions have been too marked to be mistaken. Almost as	 32.95787093215736
soon as I entered the house I singled you out as the companion of my	 37.688871069396285
future life. But before I am run away with by my feelings on this	 35.678305139179386
subject, perhaps it will be advisable for me to state my reasons for	 30.430806879462175
marrying--and moreover for coming into Hertfordshire with the design of	 38.16214879171513
selecting a wife, as I certainly did."	 33.84601407893655
The idea of Mr. Collins, with all his solemn composure, being run away	 30.6286129753614
with by his feelings, made Elizabeth so near laughing that she could not	 52.43774869323907
use the short pause he allowed in any attempt to stop him farther, and	 47.27922650834188
he continued:	 93.27868662138269
"My reasons for marrying are, first, that I think it a right thing for	 51.64559120856636

Twice has she condescended to give me her opinion (unasked too!) on this	 77.8332983020801

must marry. A clergyman like you must marry.--Chuse properly, chuse a	 44.62345337827725
gentlewoman for _my_ sake; and for your _own_, let her be an active,	 35.53268458891694
useful sort of person, not brought up high, but able to make a small	 39.55234283097975
income go a good way. This is my advice. Find such a woman as soon as	 50.21302813305131
you can, bring her to Hunsford, and I will visit her.' Allow me, by the	 38.83406115806412

"You are too hasty, Sir," she cried. "You forget that I have made no	 36.28914105585409
answer. Let me do it without farther loss of time. Accept my thanks for	 32.55982259574922

said, and shall hope to lead you to the altar ere long."	 53.71910969929634
"Upon my word, Sir," cried Elizabeth, "your hope is rather an	 63.02700321524885

one of those young ladies (if such young ladies there are) who are so	 47.86020369811296

situation."	 2547.2571975390756
"Were it certain that Lady Catherine would think so," said Mr. Collins	 40.204371253076964

disapprove of you. And you may be certain that when I have the honour of	 13.768097923618788

believing what I say. I wish you very happy and very rich, and by	 50.45610037093321

feelings with regard to my family, and may take possession of Longbourn	 49.78018499193454

considered, therefore, as finally settled." And rising as she thus	 82.26464347714386

addressed her,	 160.83793760940156

know it to be the established custom of your sex to reject a man on the	 24.34146778659188

female character."	 234.9023973739822

truth from her heart."	 68.59263089184931
"You are uniformly charming!" cried he, with an air of awkward	 39.96729234345541
gallantry; "and I am persuaded that when sanctioned by the express	 36.83852931599292

To such perseverance in wilful self-deception Elizabeth would make no	 43.085280112388524
reply, and immediately and in silence withdrew; determined, if he	 81.44763249278013
persisted in considering her repeated refusals as flattering	 42.603658044333805

not be mistaken for the affectation and coquetry of an elegant female.	 18.5058752053963
CHAPTER XX.	 12.911953956184709
Mr. Collins was not left long to the silent contemplation of his	 24.547146380076466
successful love; for Mrs. Bennet, having dawdled about in the vestibule	 37.4464579055588
to watch for the end of the conference, no sooner saw Elizabeth open the	 20.380415813828286
door and with quick step pass her towards the staircase, than she	 43.09754421252456
entered the breakfast-room, and congratulated both him and herself in	 26.344209009787736
warm terms on the happy prospect of their nearer connection. Mr. Collins	 43.314323155118245

proceeded to relate the particulars of their interview, with the result	 24.850746203899046
of which he trusted he had every reason to be satisfied, since the	 24.782372506948697

glad to be equally satisfied that her daughter had meant to encourage	 25.99503629495807

"Pardon me for interrupting you, Madam," cried Mr. Collins; "but if she	 42.5965062478197

naturally looks for happiness in the marriage state. If therefore she	 68.90483237302607
actually persists in rejecting my suit, perhaps it were better not to	 50.69086413229539

"Sir, you quite misunderstand me," said Mrs. Bennet, alarmed. "Lizzy is	 37.75234238905098

not have him, and if you do not make haste he will change his mind and	 34.68147867507228

Mr. Bennet raised his eyes from his book as she entered, and fixed them	 41.175449375400966
on her face with a calm unconcern which was not in the least altered by	 13.009818923786021

"I have not the pleasure of understanding you," said he, when she had	 19.358167614682692

"And what am I to do on the occasion?--It seems an hopeless business."	 69.55301996745958
"Speak to Lizzy about it yourself. Tell her that you insist upon her	 45.06868478461075
marrying him."	 194.09689561148073
"Let her be called down. She shall hear my opinion."	 41.25970384914896
Mrs. Bennet rang the bell, and Miss Elizabeth was summoned to the	 20.9630872797944
library.	 4505.944044823731
"Come here, child," cried her father as she appeared. "I have sent for	 50.61890177111549

you an offer of marriage. Is it true?" Elizabeth replied that it was.	 27.359350364342447
"Very well--and this offer of marriage you have refused?"	 31.35126449042181

"Very well. We now come to the point. Your mother insists upon your	 66.82113692505027
accepting it. Is not it so, Mrs. Bennet?"	 30.761380424307553

Elizabeth could not but smile at such a conclusion of such a beginning;	 23.19104366236881

affair as she wished, was excessively disappointed.	 63.14309053425632

"My dear," replied her husband, "I have two small favours to request.	 40.043215785250666
First, that you will allow me the free use of my understanding on the	 26.949394361971756
present occasion; and secondly, of my room. I shall be glad to have the	 21.93573467861955
library to myself as soon as may be."	 33.67295885537105
Not yet, however, in spite of her disappointment in her husband, did	 33.89682742507773

coaxed and threatened her by turns. She endeavoured to secure Jane in	 22.444906155344764

He thought too well of himself to comprehend on what motive his cousin	 47.47924375837043
could refuse him; and though his pride was hurt, he suffered in no other	 51.749738101984704

While the family were in this confusion, Charlotte Lucas came to spend	 36.43232340154241

CHAPTER XXI.	 12.911953956184709
The discussion of Mr. Collins's offer was now nearly at an end, and	 17.21300385891841
Elizabeth had only to suffer from the uncomfortable feelings necessarily	 58.44176195000093
attending it, and occasionally from some peevish allusion of her mother.	 49.98390691706958

CHAPTER XXII.	 12.911953956184709
The Bennets were engaged to dine with the Lucases, and again during the	 30.98106587695493

good sir?--You had better neglect your relations, than run the risk of	 36.68163085499283
offending your patroness."	 63.54164459633825
"My dear sir," replied Mr. Collins, "I am particularly obliged to you	 16.46221072286555
for this friendly caution, and you may depend upon my not taking so	 23.77434886953658
material a step without her ladyship's concurrence."	 75.04438633755066
"You cannot be too much on your guard. Risk any thing rather than her	 12.823154286636578
displeasure; and if you find it likely to be raised by your coming to us	 29.29241501107264
again, which I should think exceedingly probable, stay quietly at home,	 37.69231881716009
and be satisfied that _we_ shall take no offence."	 57.00110915529716
"Believe me, my dear sir, my gratitude is warmly excited by such	 26.864232258104202
affectionate attention; and depend upon it, you will speedily receive	 42.877224586208605
from me a letter of thanks for this, as well as for every other mark of	 31.16927621360259
your regard during my stay in Hertfordshire. As for my fair cousins,	 32.82765723273273
though my absence may not be long enough to render it necessary, I shall	 25.26497630409066
now take the liberty of wishing them health and happiness, not excepting	 44.43032952212756
my cousin Elizabeth."	 209.1570002678304
With proper civilities the ladies then withdrew; all of them equally	 50.32318299958001
surprised to find that he meditated a quick return. Mrs. Bennet wished	 48.1059996141938
to understand by it that he thought of paying his addresses to one of	 37.283927767420494
her younger girls, and Mary might have been prevailed on to accept him.	 23.548195613971323
She rated his abilities much higher than any of the others; there was a	 32.48241958353399
solidity in his reflections which often struck her, and though by no	 26.6683815911545
means so clever as herself, she thought that if encouraged to read and	 67.81194111836756
improve himself by such an example as her's, he might become a very	 32.91755116765668
agreeable companion. But on the following morning, every hope of this	 39.69477592592807
kind was done away. Miss Lucas called soon after breakfast, and in a	 44.91283594842187
private conference with Elizabeth related the event of the day before.	 26.535056114048214
The possibility of Mr. Collins's fancying himself in love with her	 21.782840937506766
friend had once occurred to Elizabeth within the last day or two; but	 24.642535278875673
that Charlotte could encourage him, seemed almost as far from	 39.70960932675813
possibility as that she could encourage him herself, and her	 42.706730278521185
astonishment was consequently so great as to overcome at first the	 45.667039895476485
bounds of decorum, and she could not help crying out,	 11.786240085696344
"Engaged to Mr. Collins! my dear Charlotte,--impossible!"	 66.65181515351789
The steady countenance which Miss Lucas had commanded in telling her	 26.649496985278525
story, gave way to a momentary confusion here on receiving so direct a	 40.86216887002123
reproach; though, as it was no more than she expected, she soon	 45.568154478883145
regained her composure, and calmly replied,	 51.41178658020541
"Why should you be surprised, my dear Eliza?--Do you think it incredible	 31.383925606403423
that Mr. Collins should be able to procure any woman's good opinion,	 22.637453374873004
because he was not so happy as to succeed with you?"	 22.41273844990881
But Elizabeth had now recollected herself, and making a strong effort	 28.561709403403253
for it, was able to assure her with tolerable firmness that the prospect	 42.45125463532716
of their relationship was highly grateful to her, and that she wished	 23.43872202781909
her all imaginable happiness.	 170.18843505689102
"I see what you are feeling," replied Charlotte,--"you must be	 43.518853723665224
surprised, very much surprised,--so lately as Mr. Collins was wishing to	 56.62498380612002
marry you. But when you have had time to think it all over, I hope you	 36.090612661345396
will be satisfied with what I have done. I am not romantic you know. I	 21.04968569395313
never was. I ask only a comfortable home; and considering Mr. Collins's	 69.47958521223647
character, connections, and situation in life, I am convinced that my	 32.856980312667716
chance of happiness with him is as fair, as most people can boast on	 55.10609200290604
entering the marriage state."	 64.48096240351822
Elizabeth quietly answered "Undoubtedly;"--and after an awkward pause,	 33.37913210902039
they returned to the rest of the family. Charlotte did not stay much	 18.88330307165422
longer, and Elizabeth was then left to reflect on what she had heard. It	 27.888034772538084
was a long time before she became at all reconciled to the idea of so	 28.894315728102583
unsuitable a match. The strangeness of Mr. Collins's making two offers	 28.25533839860559
of marriage within three days, was nothing in comparison of his being	 34.823018983614055
now accepted. She had always felt that Charlotte's opinion of matrimony	 36.91426388020768
was not exactly like her own, but she could not have supposed it	 14.55697243014609
possible that when called into action, she would have sacrificed every	 70.869234923066
better feeling to worldly advantage. Charlotte the wife of Mr. Collins,	 57.71688688535053
was a most humiliating picture!--And to the pang of a friend disgracing	 16.68636630353456
herself and sunk in her esteem, was added the distressing conviction	 33.03713096883835
that it was impossible for that friend to be tolerably happy in the lot	 21.166824655018733
she had chosen.	 32.074430812650974
CHAPTER XXIII.	 12.911953956184709
Elizabeth was sitting with her mother and sisters, reflecting on what	 28.338739281374973
she had heard, and doubting whether she were authorised to mention it,	 35.144695862417464
when Sir William Lucas himself appeared, sent by his daughter to	 44.17149019580987
announce her engagement to the family. With many compliments to them,	 24.139494642578097
and much self-gratulation on the prospect of a connection between the	 22.76474807426624
houses, he unfolded the matter,--to an audience not merely wondering,	 32.616617265567065
but incredulous; for Mrs. Bennet, with more perseverance than	 27.13861240398382
politeness, protested he must be entirely mistaken, and Lydia, always	 44.45518606777053
unguarded and often uncivil, boisterously exclaimed,	 35.571867926371496
"Good Lord! Sir William, how can you tell such a story?--Do not you know	 27.973978764879316
that Mr. Collins wants to marry Lizzy?"	 35.43197122213596
Nothing less than the complaisance of a courtier could have borne	 11.224757375993859
without anger such treatment; but Sir William's good breeding carried	 43.429955334551266
him through it all; and though he begged leave to be positive as to the	 32.72433647670504
truth of his information, he listened to all their impertinence with the	 36.86401504642193
most forbearing courtesy.	 26.70388821487571
Elizabeth, feeling it incumbent on her to relieve him from so unpleasant	 45.11264741756568
a situation, now put herself forward to confirm his account, by	 50.335983359250505
mentioning her prior knowledge of it from Charlotte herself; and	 50.999386214124996
endeavoured to put a stop to the exclamations of her mother and sisters,	 20.8339684647981
by the earnestness of her congratulations to Sir William, in which she	 14.779819584612577
was readily joined by Jane, and by making a variety of remarks on the	 23.868389555145573
happiness that might be expected from the match, the excellent character	 42.84102014824376
of Mr. Collins, and the convenient distance of Hunsford from London.	 33.58156668081714
Mrs. Bennet was in fact too much overpowered to say a great deal while	 17.189975344401763
Sir William remained; but no sooner had he left them than her feelings	 29.828427111057934
found a rapid vent. In the first place, she persisted in disbelieving	 26.280145349138177
the whole of the matter; secondly, she was very sure that Mr. Collins	 20.715319527724134
had been taken in; thirdly, she trusted that they would never be happy	 24.7509108589374
together; and fourthly, that the match might be broken off. Two	 35.314573675677515
inferences, however, were plainly deduced from the whole; one, that	 58.50818082950194
Elizabeth was the real cause of all the mischief; and the other, that	 17.776429995813633
she herself had been barbarously used by them all; and on these two	 73.95359426540381
points she principally dwelt during the rest of the day. Nothing could	 20.46476862458842
console and nothing appease her.--Nor did that day wear out her	 164.75416951962276
resentment. A week elapsed before she could see Elizabeth without	 96.88892476739993
scolding her, a month passed away before she could speak to Sir William	 23.301856882008217
or Lady Lucas without being rude, and many months were gone before she	 51.30938600911329
could at all forgive their daughter.	 58.92076584605095
Mr. Bennet's emotions were much more tranquil on the occasion, and such	 27.308912635162994
as he did experience he pronounced to be of a most agreeable sort; for	 26.18112275031067
it gratified him, he said, to discover that Charlotte Lucas, whom he had	 23.327928000387082
been used to think tolerably sensible, was as foolish as his wife, and	 32.96886870228775
more foolish than his daughter!	 35.54345255790932
Jane confessed herself a little surprised at the match; but she said	 65.95060224679871
less of her astonishment than of her earnest desire for their happiness;	 81.50031887286914
nor could Elizabeth persuade her to consider it as improbable. Kitty and	 49.29766144620773
Lydia were far from envying Miss Lucas, for Mr. Collins was only a	 32.683464010668324
clergyman; and it affected them in no other way than as a piece of news	 32.64795349326911
to spread at Meryton.	 43.38453104836667
Lady Lucas could not be insensible of triumph on being able to retort on	 21.323759759538962
Mrs. Bennet the comfort of having a daughter well married; and she	 38.42538306184213
called at Longbourn rather oftener than usual to say how happy she was,	 27.367854019091336
though Mrs. Bennet's sour looks and ill-natured remarks might have been	 25.962723126688566
enough to drive happiness away.	 57.134113271212094
Between Elizabeth and Charlotte there was a restraint which kept them	 31.710824943597586
mutually silent on the subject; and Elizabeth felt persuaded that no	 16.85660668205671
real confidence could ever subsist between them again. Her	 73.47951840110457
disappointment in Charlotte made her turn with fonder regard to her	 67.80670649471521
sister, of whose rectitude and delicacy she was sure her opinion could	 105.06230672450762
never be shaken, and for whose happiness she grew daily more anxious, as	 73.35615824480504
Bingley had now been gone a week, and nothing was heard of his return.	 42.474949541092926
Jane had sent Caroline an early answer to her letter, and was counting	 23.160204292480618
the days till she might reasonably hope to hear again. The promised	 60.93851510423915
letter of thanks from Mr. Collins arrived on Tuesday, addressed to their	 46.965076283679586
father, and written with all the solemnity of gratitude which a	 43.353722434908285
twelvemonth's abode in the family might have prompted. After discharging	 34.44656053366987
his conscience on that head, he proceeded to inform them, with many	 20.92250081606629
rapturous expressions, of his happiness in having obtained the affection	 38.84073037948469
of their amiable neighbour, Miss Lucas, and then explained that it was	 14.823871692321934
merely with the view of enjoying her society that he had been so ready	 28.436736771989153
to close with their kind wish of seeing him again at Longbourn, whither	 41.42970888756827
he hoped to be able to return on Monday fortnight; for Lady Catherine,	 20.305687202006357
he added, so heartily approved his marriage, that she wished it to take	 40.05654419419967
place as soon as possible, which he trusted would be an unanswerable	 17.046838387346288
argument with his amiable Charlotte to name an early day for making him	 73.75085078448092
the happiest of men.	 15.865039502241713
Mr. Collins's return into Hertfordshire was no longer a matter of	 17.22209762404653
pleasure to Mrs. Bennet. On the contrary she was as much disposed to	 17.63153508975243
complain of it as her husband.--It was very strange that he should come	 23.23653927982393
to Longbourn instead of to Lucas Lodge; it was also very inconvenient	 20.239177319061582
and exceedingly troublesome.--She hated having visitors in the house	 58.52909151662775
while her health was so indifferent, and lovers were of all people the	 67.17349829318054
most disagreeable. Such were the gentle murmurs of Mrs. Bennet, and they	 56.45280152725797
gave way only to the greater distress of Mr. Bingley's continued	 41.470746421678676
absence.	 3779.3937401499625
Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this subject. Day after	 83.54426124010313
day passed away without bringing any other tidings of him than the	 34.82037643415864
report which shortly prevailed in Meryton of his coming no more to	 56.28378628660471
Netherfield the whole winter; a report which highly incensed Mrs.	 46.48062943464216
Bennet, and which she never failed to contradict as a most scandalous	 36.74051816307551
falsehood.	 8.776652323334677
Even Elizabeth began to fear--not that Bingley was indifferent--but that	 46.45375427026409
his sisters would be successful in keeping him away. Unwilling as she	 35.23734338789911
was to admit an idea so destructive of Jane's happiness, and so	 39.49395289892893
dishonourable to the stability of her lover, she could not prevent its	 11.539914085796285
frequently recurring. The united efforts of his two unfeeling sisters	 55.37256362765078
and of his overpowering friend, assisted by the attractions of Miss	 42.59102242639996
Darcy and the amusements of London, might be too much, she feared, for	 33.311260357976586
the strength of his attachment.	 13.45639815371866
As for Jane, _her_ anxiety under this suspence was, of course, more	 60.609736775164436
painful than Elizabeth's; but whatever she felt she was desirous of	 54.399354818913054
concealing, and between herself and Elizabeth, therefore, the subject	 59.17575236764638
was never alluded to. But as no such delicacy restrained her mother, an	 80.33850994199989
hour seldom passed in which she did not talk of Bingley, express her	 16.349139113289027
impatience for his arrival, or even require Jane to confess that if he	 55.75350134259676
did not come back, she should think herself very ill used. It needed all	 32.948632286729904
Jane's steady mildness to bear these attacks with tolerable	 78.63951796464552
tranquillity.	 2349.196044979379
Mr. Collins returned most punctually on the Monday fortnight, but his	 17.31565614962975
reception at Longbourn was not quite so gracious as it had been on his	 32.09862385537992
first introduction. He was too happy, however, to need much attention;	 64.98316391871094
and luckily for the others, the business of love-making relieved them	 35.941617625430474
from a great deal of his company. The chief of every day was spent by	 19.10504236440305
him at Lucas Lodge, and he sometimes returned to Longbourn only in time	 90.20906981251854
to make an apology for his absence before the family went to bed.	 28.63948655756078
Mrs. Bennet was really in a most pitiable state. The very mention of any	 21.164632717848143
thing concerning the match threw her into an agony of ill humour, and	 30.28829238307207
wherever she went she was sure of hearing it talked of. The sight of	 46.17628512232017
Miss Lucas was odious to her. As her successor in that house, she	 37.48734231811955
regarded her with jealous abhorrence. Whenever Charlotte came to see	 24.18219820614655
them she concluded her to be anticipating the hour of possession; and	 43.871729898229084
whenever she spoke in a low voice to Mr. Collins, was convinced that	 16.601699084798035
they were talking of the Longbourn estate, and resolving to turn herself	 21.7234461237496
and her daughters out of the house, as soon as Mr. Bennet were dead. She	 21.422006403550267
complained bitterly of all this to her husband.	 25.12311974680976
"Indeed, Mr. Bennet," said she, "it is very hard to think that Charlotte	 16.587905974620522
Lucas should ever be mistress of this house, that I should be forced to	 27.57469807348929
make way for _her_, and live to see her take my place in it!"	 47.560838814038895
"My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope for	 18.23833120276299
better things. Let us flatter ourselves that _I_ may be the survivor."	 16.92453625547963
This was not very consoling to Mrs. Bennet, and, therefore, instead of	 19.6154030798137
making any answer, she went on as before,	 81.84104720147
"I cannot bear to think that they should have all this estate. If it was	 16.791923585142804
not for the entail I should not mind it."	 30.734724900811553
"What should not you mind?"	 72.29638333678066
"I should not mind any thing at all."	 13.951311900165067
"Let us be thankful that you are preserved from a state of such	 22.54336835805709
insensibility."	 2697.3431099943446
"I never can be thankful, Mr. Bennet, for any thing about the entail.	 33.122759635608354
How any one could have the conscience to entail away an estate from	 44.483335240238176
one's own daughters I cannot understand; and all for the sake of Mr.	 75.54002937125834
Collins too!--Why should _he_ have it more than anybody else?"	 62.17335075703383
"I leave it to yourself to determine," said Mr. Bennet.	 44.14528957930257
END OF VOL. I.	 36.663034011004925

PRIDE & PREJUDICE.	 34.24156922046106
CHAPTER I.	 254.0372352719546
Miss Bingley's letter arrived, and put an end to doubt. The very first	 29.034893861971128
sentence conveyed the assurance of their being all settled in London for	 47.85593239186921
the winter, and concluded with her brother's regret at not having had	 50.909628367532136
time to pay his respects to his friends in Hertfordshire before he left	 28.864926383256673
the country.	 52.207351272420375
Hope was over, entirely over; and when Jane could attend to the rest of	 42.029301740901055
the letter, she found little, except the professed affection of the	 25.554938176726562
writer, that could give her any comfort. Miss Darcy's praise occupied	 69.92311775718974
the chief of it. Her many attractions were again dwelt on, and Caroline	 53.247830899930726
boasted joyfully of their increasing intimacy, and ventured to predict	 10.382674045505018
the accomplishment of the wishes which had been unfolded in her former	 15.835698622461386
letter. She wrote also with great pleasure of her brother's being an	 26.90537148322334
inmate of Mr. Darcy's house, and mentioned with raptures, some plans of	 16.43192032158012
the latter with regard to new furniture.	 75.78618257004436
Elizabeth, to whom Jane very soon communicated the chief of all this,	 47.7161204523123
heard it in silent indignation. Her heart was divided between concern	 44.64436087559872
for her sister, and resentment against all the others. To Caroline's	 30.412316137092933
assertion of her brother's being partial to Miss Darcy she paid no	 13.379137778838263
credit. That he was really fond of Jane, she doubted no more than she	 21.201145889945366
had ever done; and much as she had always been disposed to like him, she	 31.889490203311848
could not think without anger, hardly without contempt, on that easiness	 52.27009920071123
of temper, that want of proper resolution which now made him the slave	 31.613589066541458
of his designing friends, and led him to sacrifice his own happiness to	 34.67203778052895
the caprice of their inclinations. Had his own happiness, however, been	 22.680707368512966
the only sacrifice, he might have been allowed to sport with it in what	 26.157192317111058
ever manner he thought best; but her sister's was involved in it, as she	 67.88713613567154
thought he must be sensible himself. It was a subject, in short, on	 42.27476344840029
which reflection would be long indulged, and must be unavailing. She	 34.103278700996775
could think of nothing else, and yet whether Bingley's regard had really	 52.19084453671285
died away, or were suppressed by his friends' interference; whether he	 29.856671027354565
had been aware of Jane's attachment, or whether it had escaped his	 23.463779539736127
observation; whichever were the case, though her opinion of him must be	 52.710210249722685
materially affected by the difference, her sister's situation remained	 41.42063715403635
the same, her peace equally wounded.	 101.07969129631483
A day or two passed before Jane had courage to speak of her feelings to	 21.599901215568295
Elizabeth; but at last on Mrs. Bennet's leaving them together, after a	 37.834854475504095
longer irritation than usual about Netherfield and its master, she could	 53.43796684289008
not help saying,	 41.003022727975086
"Oh! that my dear mother had more command over herself; she can have no	 57.082956828640825
idea of the pain she gives me by her continual reflections on him. But I	 45.01784692687915
will not repine. It cannot last long. He will be forgot, and we shall	 42.46245104156304
all be as we were before."	 130.35689149685595
Elizabeth looked at her sister with incredulous solicitude, but said	 33.43039299080935
nothing.	 421.23545976312556
"You doubt me," cried Jane, slightly colouring; "indeed you have no	 41.388011777335194
reason. He may live in my memory as the most amiable man of my	 31.503110224335096
acquaintance, but that is all. I have nothing either to hope or fear,	 60.77898750434227
and nothing to reproach him with. Thank God! I have not _that_ pain. A	 50.192906410759775
little time therefore.--I shall certainly try to get the better."	 88.75582337240844
With a stronger voice she soon added, "I have this comfort immediately,	 41.853528178728546
that it has not been more than an error of fancy on my side, and that it	 20.82902078806314
has done no harm to any one but myself."	 43.781045628997454
"My dear Jane!" exclaimed Elizabeth, "you are too good. Your sweetness	 26.093973025161873
and disinterestedness are really angelic; I do not know what to say to	 19.005183334618263
you. I feel as if I had never done you justice, or loved you as you	 41.83851582133034
deserve."	 7215.009090329725
Miss Bennet eagerly disclaimed all extraordinary merit, and threw back	 37.81482572103916
the praise on her sister's warm affection.	 47.71527396844967
"Nay," said Elizabeth, "this is not fair. _You_ wish to think all the	 53.347705397171225
world respectable, and are hurt if I speak ill of any body. _I_ only	 42.38004913891494
want to think _you_ perfect, and you set yourself against it. Do not be	 45.264388588559385
afraid of my running into any excess, of my encroaching on your	 34.874900300250445
privilege of universal good will. You need not. There are few people	 20.54968076221959
whom I really love, and still fewer of whom I think well. The more I see	 79.00967601801177
of the world, the more am I dissatisfied with it; and every day confirms	 91.79743815202146
my belief of the inconsistency of all human characters, and of the	 19.02069381029683
little dependence that can be placed on the appearance of either merit	 38.10496998563096
or sense. I have met with two instances lately; one I will not mention;	 59.4840050214529
the other is Charlotte's marriage. It is unaccountable! in every view it	 30.854553759051022
is unaccountable!"	 76.02745322177897
"My dear Lizzy, do not give way to such feelings as these. They will	 17.986678549674483
ruin your happiness. You do not make allowance enough for difference of	 34.00309694441255
situation and temper. Consider Mr. Collins's respectability, and	 66.01796302389995
Charlotte's prudent, steady character. Remember that she is one of a	 39.637066728321685
large family; that as to fortune, it is a most eligible match; and be	 42.343786990004254
ready to believe, for every body's sake, that she may feel something	 62.92693612144961
like regard and esteem for our cousin."	 81.2559798207355
"To oblige you, I would try to believe almost any thing, but no one else	 28.96390727748593
could be benefited by such a belief as this; for were I persuaded that	 31.20215386551552
Charlotte had any regard for him, I should only think worse of her	 37.07741011264144
understanding, than I now do of her heart. My dear Jane, Mr. Collins is	 58.61871829569589
a conceited, pompous, narrow-minded, silly man; you know he is, as well	 47.68679156158383
as I do; and you must feel, as well as I do, that the woman who marries	 36.18897585389149
him, cannot have a proper way of thinking. You shall not defend her,	 45.02180017524821
though it is Charlotte Lucas. You shall not, for the sake of one	 31.663033384707056
individual, change the meaning of principle and integrity, nor	 35.143385023596096
endeavour to persuade yourself or me, that selfishness is prudence, and	 41.944305372325765
insensibility of danger, security for happiness."	 124.05089483610564
"I must think your language too strong in speaking of both," replied	 43.536828377953626
Jane, "and I hope you will be convinced of it, by seeing them happy	 34.8700288698479
together. But enough of this. You alluded to something else. You	 92.63628648300849
mentioned _two_ instances. I cannot misunderstand you, but I intreat	 50.776416631298446
you, dear Lizzy, not to pain me by thinking _that person_ to blame, and	 41.32037369041579
saying your opinion of him is sunk. We must not be so ready to fancy	 29.969453457958107
ourselves intentionally injured. We must not expect a lively young man	 31.833509044931404
to be always so guarded and circumspect. It is very often nothing but	 22.255133857035517
our own vanity that deceives us. Women fancy admiration means more than	 135.51306784342893
it does."	 124.4987597509137
"And men take care that they should."	 37.04212614075165
"If it is designedly done, they cannot be justified; but I have no idea	 25.722863557785
of there being so much design in the world as some persons imagine."	 49.64866004572878
"I am far from attributing any part of Mr. Bingley's conduct to design,"	 25.326882720003812

unhappy, there may be error, and there may be misery. Thoughtlessness,	 31.411554090365964
want of attention to other people's feelings, and want of resolution,	 48.43325620216684

Mrs. Bennet still continued to wonder and repine at his returning no	 26.998149405811773

Mr. Bennet treated the matter differently. "So, Lizzy," said he one day,	 36.80664517783592
"your sister is crossed in love I find. I congratulate her. Next to	 47.08515694874872

that kind may befal you, you have an affectionate mother who will always	 29.49481428873615

Mr. Wickham's society was of material service in dispelling the gloom,	 38.49598767849466
which the late perverse occurrences had thrown on many of the Longbourn	 47.84512147540336

CHAPTER II.	 12.911953956184709
After a week spent in professions of love and schemes of felicity, Mr.	 33.51755192981272
Collins was called from his amiable Charlotte by the arrival of	 65.75916188715478
Saturday. The pain of separation, however, might be alleviated on his	 29.78870091894491

to hope, that shortly after his next return into Hertfordshire, the day	 54.7192211925363

of his relations at Longbourn with as much solemnity as before; wished	 48.74522035593127
his fair cousins health and happiness again, and promised their father	 26.948501949387808
another letter of thanks.	 111.00976176462564
On the following Monday, Mrs. Bennet had the pleasure of receiving her	 25.183526752144502
brother and his wife, who came as usual to spend the Christmas at	 38.76089371882949
Longbourn. Mr. Gardiner was a sensible, gentleman-like man, greatly	 36.236479863222975

The first part of Mrs. Gardiner's business on her arrival, was to	 20.024832245455894
distribute her presents and describe the newest fashions. When this was	 29.422057932293423
done, she had a less active part to play. It became her turn to listen.	 42.97142924766534
Mrs. Bennet had many grievances to relate, and much to complain of. They	 59.894828244738605
had all been very ill-used since she last saw her sister. Two of her	 53.03242128558267
girls had been on the point of marriage, and after all there was nothing	 36.849782199643684
in it.	 92.71026957922234

Bingley, if she could. But, Lizzy! Oh, sister! it is very hard to think	 31.508214801507673

Mrs. Gardiner, to whom the chief of this news had been given before, in	 29.44687856793006

conversation.	 760.6645577472327

CHAPTER III.	 12.911953956184709
Mrs. Gardiner's caution to Elizabeth was punctually and kindly given on	 31.208931497824455
the first favourable opportunity of speaking to her alone; after	 21.751568175916024

CHAPTER IV.	 12.911953956184709
With no greater events than these in the Longbourn family, and otherwise	 35.90732039004966

CHAPTER V.	 12.911953956184709

CHAPTER VI.	 12.911953956184709
Mr. Collins's triumph in consequence of this invitation was complete.	 20.028664010122984

CHAPTER VII.	 12.911953956184709
Sir William staid only a week at Hunsford; but his visit was long enough	 21.723730218552806

CHAPTER VIII.	 12.911953956184709

CHAPTER X.	 12.911953956184709

CHAPTER XI.	 12.911953956184709
When they were gone, Elizabeth, as if intending to exasperate herself as	 23.477957843093524
